<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Cost of electricity by source</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the price of electricity, see <a href="/wiki/Electricity_pricing" title="Electricity pricing">Electricity pricing</a>.</div>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power_generation" title="Electrical power generation">electrical power generation</a>, the distinct ways of generating <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> incur significantly different costs. Calculations of these costs can be made at the point of connection to a load or to the electricity grid. The cost is typically given per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hour</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt-hour" title="Megawatt-hour">megawatt-hour</a>. It includes the initial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_(finance)" title="Capital (finance)">capital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annual_effective_discount_rate" title="Annual effective discount rate">discount rate</a>, as well as the costs of continuous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_costs" title="Operating costs">operation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maintenance,_repair,_and_operations" title="Maintenance, repair, and operations">maintenance</a>. This type of calculation assists policymakers, researchers and others to guide discussions and decision making.
</p><p>The <b>levelized cost of energy</b> (<b>LCOE</b>) is a measure of a power source which allows to compare different methods of electricity generation on a consistent basis. It is an economic assessment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Average_total_cost" title="Average total cost">average total cost</a> to build and operate a power-generating asset over its lifetime divided by the total energy output of the asset over that lifetime. The LCOE can also be regarded as the average minimum price at which electricity must be sold in order to <a href="/wiki/Break-even_(economics)" title="Break-even (economics)">break-even</a> over the lifetime of the project.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_factors">Cost factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Cost factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While calculating costs, several internal cost factors have to be considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Note the use of "costs," which is not the actual selling price, since this can be affected by a variety of factors such as subsidies and taxes:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_costs" title="Capital costs">Capital costs</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">waste</a> disposal and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">decommissioning</a> costs for nuclear energy) – tend to be low for <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power stations</a>; high for wind turbines, solar PV (photovoltaics); very high for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waste_to_energy" title="Waste to energy">waste to energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">wave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a>.</li>
<li>Fuel costs – high for fossil fuel and biomass sources, low for nuclear, and zero for many renewables. Fuel costs can vary somewhat unpredictably over the life of the generating equipment, due to political and other factors.</li>
<li>Factors such as the costs of waste (and associated issues) and different insurance costs are not included in the following: Works power, own use or <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_load" title="Parasitic load">parasitic load</a> – that is, the portion of generated power actually used to run the station's pumps and fans has to be allowed for.</li></ul>
<p>To evaluate the total cost of production of electricity, the streams of costs are converted to a <a href="/wiki/Net_present_value" title="Net present value">net present value</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Time_value_of_money" title="Time value of money">time value of money</a>. These costs are all brought together using <a href="/wiki/Discounted_cash_flow" title="Discounted cash flow">discounted cash flow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Levelized_cost_of_electricity">Levelized cost of electricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Levelized cost of electricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The levelized cost of electricity (LCOE), also known as Levelized Energy Cost (LEC), is the net present value of the unit-cost of electricity over the lifetime of a generating asset. It is often taken as a proxy for the average price that the generating asset must receive in a market to break even over its lifetime. It is a first-order economic assessment of the cost competitiveness of an electricity-generating system that incorporates all costs over its lifetime: initial investment, operations and maintenance, cost of fuel, <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_capital" title="Cost of capital">cost of capital</a>.
</p><p>The levelized cost is that value for which an equal-valued fixed revenue delivered over the life of the asset's generating profile would cause the project to break even. This can be roughly calculated as the net present value of all costs over the lifetime of the asset divided by the total electrical energy output of the asset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The levelized cost of electricity (LCOE) is given by:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {LCOE} ={\frac {\text{sum of costs over lifetime}}{\text{sum of electrical energy produced over lifetime}}}={\frac {\sum _{t=1}^{n}{\frac {I_{t}+M_{t}+F_{t}}{\left({1+r}\right)^{t}}}}{\sum _{t=1}^{n}{\frac {E_{t}}{\left({1+r}\right)^{t}}}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {LCOE} ={\frac {\text{sum of costs over lifetime}}{\text{sum of electrical energy produced over lifetime}}}={\frac {\sum _{t=1}^{n}{\frac {I_{t}+M_{t}+F_{t}}{\left({1+r}\right)^{t}}}}{\sum _{t=1}^{n}{\frac {E_{t}}{\left({1+r}\right)^{t}}}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="
\mathrm{LCOE} = \frac{\text{sum of costs over lifetime}}{\text{sum of electrical energy produced over lifetime}} = \frac{\sum_{t=1}^{n} \frac{ I_t + M_t + F_t}{\left({1+r}\right)^t} }{\sum_{t=1}^{n} \frac{E_t}{\left({1+r}\right)^{t}} }
" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57028f3c41c681553c77ed2111a69a6f08290b4e" style="vertical-align: -4.505ex; width:75.276ex; height:10.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><table style="width:100%; margin-top: 12px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td><i><b>I<sub>t</sub></b></i></td>
<td>:</td>
<td>investment expenditures in the year <i>t</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i><b>M<sub>t</sub></b></i></td>
<td>:</td>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operations_and_maintenance" title="Operations and maintenance">operations and maintenance</a> expenditures in the year <i>t</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i><b>F<sub>t</sub></b></i></td>
<td>:</td>
<td>fuel expenditures in the year <i>t</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i><b>E<sub>t</sub></b></i></td>
<td>:</td>
<td>electrical energy generated in the year <i>t</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i><b>r</b></i></td>
<td>:</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Annual_effective_discount_rate" title="Annual effective discount rate">discount rate</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i><b>n</b></i></td>
<td>:</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Service_life" title="Service life">expected lifetime</a> of system or power station
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;line-height: 110%; padding: 14px 0; width: 60%;">Note: Some caution must be taken when using formulas for the levelized cost, as they often embody unseen assumptions, neglect effects like taxes, and may be specified in real or nominal levelized cost. For example, other versions of the above formula do not discount the electricity stream.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div></dd></dl>
<p>Typically the LCOE is calculated over the design lifetime of a plant, which is usually 20 to 40 years, and given in the units of currency per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hour</a> or megawatt-day, for example AUD/kWh or EUR/kWh or per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt-hour" title="Megawatt-hour">megawatt-hour</a>, for example AUD/MWh (as tabulated below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> However, care should be taken in comparing different LCOE studies and the sources of the information as the LCOE for a given energy source is highly dependent on the assumptions, financing terms and technological deployment analyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Branker_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Branker-6">[6]</a></sup> In particular, assumption of <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> has significant impact on the calculation of LCOE. Thus, a key requirement for the analysis is a clear statement of the applicability of the analysis based on justified assumptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Branker_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Branker-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many scholars,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (July 2015)">specify</span></a></i>]</sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Paul_Joskow" title="Paul Joskow">Paul Joskow</a>, have described limits to the "levelized cost of electricity" metric for comparing new generating sources. In particular, LCOE ignores time effects associated with matching production to demand. This happens at two levels:
</p>
<ul><li>Dispatchability, the ability of a generating system to come online, go offline, or ramp up or down, quickly as demand swings.</li>
<li>The extent to which the availability profile matches or conflicts with the market demand profile.</li></ul>
<p>Thermally lethargic technologies like coal and nuclear are physically incapable of fast ramping. Capital intensive technologies such as wind, solar, and nuclear are economically disadvantaged unless generating at maximum availability since the LCOE is nearly all sunk-cost capital investment. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermittent" title="Intermittent">Intermittent</a> power sources, such as wind and solar, may incur extra costs associated with needing to have storage or backup generation available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> At the same time, intermittent sources can be competitive if they are available to produce when demand and prices are highest, such as solar during summertime mid-day peaks seen in hot countries where <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> is a major consumer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Branker_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Branker-6">[6]</a></sup> Despite these time limitations, leveling costs is often a necessary prerequisite for making comparisons on an equal footing before demand profiles are considered, and the levelized-cost metric is widely used for comparing technologies at the margin, where grid implications of new generation can be neglected.
</p><p>Another limitation of the LCOE metric is the influence of <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">conservation</a> (EEC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bronski2014-8">[8]</a></sup>
EEC has caused the electricity demand of many countries to remain flat or decline. Considering only the LCOE for utility scale plants will tend to maximise generation and risks overestimating required generation due to efficiency, thus "lowballing" their LCOE. For solar systems installed at the point of end use, it is more economical to invest in EEC first, then solar (resulting in a smaller required solar system than what would be needed without the EEC measures). However, designing a solar system on the basis of LCOE would cause the smaller system LCOE to increase (as the energy generation [measured in kWh] drops faster than the system cost [$]). The whole of system life cycle cost should be considered, not just the LCOE of the energy source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bronski2014-8">[8]</a></sup> LCOE is not as relevant to end-users than other financial considerations such as income, cashflow, mortgage, leases, rent, and electricity bills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bronski2014-8">[8]</a></sup> Comparing solar investments in relation to these can make it easier for end-users to make a decision, or using cost-benefit calculations "and/or an asset’s capacity value or contribution to peak on a system or circuit level".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bronski2014-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Avoided_cost">Avoided cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Avoided cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The US <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> has recommended that levelized costs of non-<a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable</a> sources such as wind or solar may be better compared to the avoided energy cost rather than to the LCOE of dispatchable sources such as fossil fuels or geothermal. This is because introduction of fluctuating power sources may or may not avoid capital and maintenance costs of backup dispatchable sources. Levelized Avoided Cost of Energy (LACE) is the avoided costs from other sources divided by the annual yearly output of the non-dispatchable source. However, the avoided cost is much harder to calculate accurately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eia.gov_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-eia.gov-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marginal_cost_of_electricity">Marginal cost of electricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Marginal cost of electricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A more accurate economic assessment might be the <a href="/wiki/Marginal_cost" title="Marginal cost">marginal cost</a> of electricity. This value works by comparing the added system cost of increasing electricity generation from one source versus that from other sources of electricity generation (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merit_Order" title="Merit Order">Merit Order</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="External_costs_of_energy_sources">External costs of energy sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: External costs of energy sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_energy_industry" title="Environmental impact of the energy industry">Environmental impact of the energy industry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">Economics of new nuclear power plants</a></div>
<p>Typically pricing of electricity from various energy sources may not include all <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">external costs</a> – that is, the costs indirectly borne by society as a whole as a consequence of using that energy source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eu2012_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-eu2012-12">[12]</a></sup> These may include enabling costs, environmental impacts, usage lifespans, energy storage, recycling costs, or beyond-insurance accident effects.
</p><p>The US Energy Information Administration predicts that <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_power_station" title="Fossil fuel power station">coal and gas</a> are set to be continually used to deliver the majority of the world's electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> This is expected to result in the evacuation of millions of homes in low-lying areas, and an annual cost of hundreds of billions of dollars' worth of property damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, with a number of island nations becoming slowly submerged underwater due to rising sea levels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> massive international climate litigation lawsuits against fossil fuel users are currently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (October 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> beginning in the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>An EU funded research study known as ExternE, or <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">Externalities</a> of Energy, undertaken over the period of 1995 to 2005 found that the cost of producing electricity from coal or oil would double over its present value, and the cost of electricity production from gas would increase by 30% if external costs such as damage to the environment and to human health, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulate matter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxides" title="Nitrogen oxides">nitrogen oxides</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromium_VI" title="Chromium VI">chromium VI</a>, river water <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_soils" title="Alkali soils">alkalinity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_poisoning" title="Mercury poisoning">mercury poisoning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> emissions produced by these sources, were taken into account. It was estimated in the study that these external, downstream, fossil fuel costs amount up to 1%–2% of the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_European_Union" title="Economy of the European Union">EU’s entire Gross Domestic Product (GDP)</a>, and this was before the external cost of global warming from these sources was even included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Coal has the highest external cost in the EU, and global warming is the largest part of that cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eu2012_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-eu2012-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>A means to address a part of the external costs of fossil fuel generation is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_pricing" title="Carbon pricing">carbon pricing</a> — the method most favored by economics for reducing global-warming emissions. Carbon pricing charges those who emit carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) for their emissions. That charge, called a 'carbon price', is the amount that must be paid for the right to emit one tonne of CO<sub>2</sub> into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Carbon pricing usually takes the form of a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> or a requirement to purchase permits to emit (also called "allowances").
</p><p>Depending on the assumptions of possible accidents and their probabilites external costs for nuclear power vary significantly and can reach between 0.2 and 200 ct/kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Furthermore, nuclear power is working under an insurance framework that limits or structures accident liabilities in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Convention_on_Third_Party_Liability_in_the_Field_of_Nuclear_Energy" title="Paris Convention on Third Party Liability in the Field of Nuclear Energy">Paris convention on nuclear third-party liability</a>, the Brussels supplementary convention, and the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Civil_Liability_for_Nuclear_Damage" title="Vienna Convention on Civil Liability for Nuclear Damage">Vienna convention on civil liability for nuclear damage</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and in the U.S. the <a href="/wiki/Price%E2%80%93Anderson_Nuclear_Industries_Indemnity_Act" title="Price–Anderson Nuclear Industries Indemnity Act">Price-Anderson Act</a>. It is often argued that this potential shortfall in liability represents an external cost not included in the cost of nuclear electricity; but the cost is small, amounting to about 0.1% of the levelized cost of electricity, according to a CBO study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>These beyond-insurance costs for worst-case scenarios are not unique to nuclear power, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" title="Hydroelectric power">hydroelectric power</a> plants are similarly not fully insured against a catastrophic event such as the <a href="/wiki/Banqiao_Dam" title="Banqiao Dam">Banqiao Dam</a> disaster, where 11 million people lost their homes and from 30,000 to 200,000 people died, or large <a href="/wiki/Dam_failure" title="Dam failure">dam failures</a> in general. As private insurers base dam insurance premiums on limited scenarios, major disaster insurance in this sector is likewise provided by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because externalities are diffuse in their effect, external costs can not be measured directly, but must be estimated. One approach estimate external costs of environmental impact of electricity is the Methodological Convention of Federal Environment Agency of Germany. That method arrives at external costs of electricity from lignite at 10.75 Eurocent/kWh, from hard coal 8.94 Eurocent/kWh, from natural gas 4.91 Eurocent/kWh, from photovoltaic 1.18 Eurocent/kWh, from wind 0.26 Eurocent/kWh and from hydro 0.18 Eurocent/kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> For nuclear the Federal Environment Agency indicates no value, as different studies have results that vary by a factor of 1,000. It recommends the nuclear given the huge uncertainty, with the cost of the next inferior energy source to evaluate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Based on this recommendation the Federal Environment Agency, and with their own method, the Forum Ecological-social market economy, arrive at external environmental costs of nuclear energy at 10.7 to 34 ct/kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_cost_factors">Additional cost factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Additional cost factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Calculations often do not include wider system costs associated with each type of plant, such as long distance transmission connections to grids, or balancing and reserve costs. Calculations do not include externalities such as health damage by coal plants, nor the effect of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions on the <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean current</a> shifts. Decommissioning costs of nuclear plants are usually not included (The USA is an exception, because the cost of decommissioning is included in the price of electricity, per the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Waste_Policy_Act" title="Nuclear Waste Policy Act">Nuclear Waste Policy Act</a>), is therefore not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_cost_accounting" title="Full cost accounting">full cost accounting</a>. These types of items can be explicitly added as necessary depending on the purpose of the calculation. It has little relation to actual price of power, but assists policy makers and others to guide discussions and decision making.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>These are not minor factors but very significantly affect all responsible power decisions:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparisons_of_life-cycle_greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Comparisons of life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions">Comparisons of life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions</a> show coal, for instance, to be radically higher in terms of GHGs than any alternative. <i>Accordingly, in the analysis below, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_capture" title="Carbon capture">carbon captured</a> coal is generally treated as a separate source rather than being averaged in with other coal.</i></li>
<li>Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_concerns_with_electricity_generation" title="Environmental concerns with electricity generation">environmental concerns with electricity generation</a> include <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> and effect of coal extraction on watersheds.</li>
<li>Various human health concerns with electricity generation, including <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">smog</a>, now dominate decisions in developed nations that incur health care costs publicly. A <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> Medical School study estimates the US health costs of coal alone at between 300 and 500 billion US dollars annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>While <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission#Cost_of_electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">cost per kWh of transmission</a> varies drastically with distance, the long complex projects required to clear or even upgrade transmission routes make even attractive new supplies often uncompetitive with conservation measures (see below), because the timing of payoff must take the transmission upgrade into account.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_Global_Studies">Current Global Studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Current Global Studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Bloomberg_.282018.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bloomberg_(2018)">Bloomberg (2018)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bloomberg (2018)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bloomberg New Energy Finance estimates a "global LCOE for onshore wind [of] $55 per megawatt-hour, down 18% from the first six months of [2017], while the equivalent for solar PV without tracking systems is $70 per MWh, also down 18%." Bloomberg does not provide its global public LCOEs for fossil fuels, but it notes in India they are significantly more expensive: "BNEF is now showing benchmark LCOEs for onshore wind of just $39 per MWh, down 46% on a year ago, and for solar PV at $41, down 45%. By comparison, coal comes in at $68 per MWh, and combined-cycle gas at $93." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BloombergNE02018_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-BloombergNE02018-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="IRENA_.282018.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="IRENA_(2018)">IRENA (2018)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: IRENA (2018)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Renewable_Energy_Agency" title="International Renewable Energy Agency">International Renewable Energy Agency</a> (IRENA) released a study based on comprehensive international datasets in January 2018 which projects the fall by 2020 of the kilowatt cost of electricity from utility scale renewable projects such as onshore wind farms to a point equal or below that of electricity from conventional sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRENA118_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRENA118-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_and_Historical_Studies">Regional and Historical Studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Regional and Historical Studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</td></tr>

<td>Supercritical black coal</td>
<td>$135 – $145</td>
<td>$84 – $94
</td></tr>

<td>$162 – $205</td>

</td></tr>

<td>$111 – $122
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="808" data-file-width="1162" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png/220px-LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png/330px-LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png/440px-LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LCOEs_of_energy_generators_in_Australia_AETA_2013_Update_Figure_8.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>LCOEs by source in Australia in 2012.</div></div></div>
<p>According to various studies, the cost for wind and solar has dramatically reduced since 2006. For example, the Australian <a href="/wiki/Climate_Council" title="Climate Council">Climate Council</a> states that over the 5 years between 2009–2014 solar costs fell by 75% making them comparable to coal, and are expected to continue dropping over the next 5 years by another 45% from 2014 prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CC2014_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-CC2014-38">[38]</a></sup> They also found that wind has been cheaper than coal since 2013, and that coal and gas will become less viable as subsidies are withdrawn and there is the expectation that they will eventually have to pay the costs of pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CC2014_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-CC2014-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>A CO2CRC report, printed on the 27th of November 2015, titled <a class="external text" href="https://theconversation.com/wind-solar-coal-and-gas-to-reach-similar-costs-by-2030-report-51306" rel="nofollow">"Wind, solar, coal and gas to reach similar costs by 2030:"</a>, provides the following updated situation in Australia. "The updated LCOE analysis finds that in 2015 natural gas combined cycle and supercritical pulverised coal (both black and brown) plants have the lowest LCOEs of the technologies covered in the study. Wind is the lowest cost large-scale renewable energy source, while rooftop solar panels are competitive with retail electricity prices. By 2030 the LCOE ranges of both conventional coal and gas technologies as well as wind and large-scale solar converge to a common range of A$50 to A$100 per megawatt hour."
</p><p>An updated report, posted on the 27th of September 2017, titled "<a class="external text" href="https://theconversation.com/renewables-will-be-cheaper-than-coal-in-the-future-here-are-the-numbers-84433" rel="nofollow">Renewables will be cheaper than coal in the future. Here are the numbers</a>", indicated that a 100% renewables system is competitive with new-build supercritical (ultrasupercritical) coal, which, according to the Jacobs calculations in the report link above, would come in at around A$75(80) per MWh between 2020 and 2050. This projection for supercritical coal is consistent with other studies by the <a class="external text" href="http://www.co2crc.com.au/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/LCOE_Report_final_web.pdf" rel="nofollow">CO2CRC in 2015</a> (A$80 per MWh) and used by <a class="external text" href="https://www.csiro.au/en/Do-business/Futures/Reports/Low-Emissions-Technology-Roadmap" rel="nofollow">CSIRO in 2017</a> (A$65-80 per MWh).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(July 2015)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lectricit%C3%A9_de_France" title="Électricité de France">EDF</a> have estimated for 2011 the following costs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For the nuclear power they include the costs due to new safety investments to upgrade the French nuclear plant after the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>; the cost for those investments is estimated at 4 €/MWh. Concerning the solar power the estimate at 293 €/MWh is for a large plant capable to produce in the range of 50–100 GWh/year located in a favorable location (such as in Southern Europe). For a small household plant capable to produce typically around 3 MWh/year the cost is according to the location between 400 and 700 €/MWh. Currently solar power is by far the most expensive renewable source to produce electricity among the technologies studied,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> although increasing efficiency and longer lifespan of photovoltaic panels together with reduced production costs could make this source of energy more competitive. In 2017, cost of generation decrease to €55,5/MWh for plant between 5 and 17MWp.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="614" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png/300px-Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png/450px-Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png/600px-Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Levelized_cost_of_electricity_Germany_2018_ISE.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of the levelized cost of electricity for some newly built <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" title="Fossil-fuel power station">fossil-fuel</a> based <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power stations</a> in EuroCent per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a> (Germany, 2018)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraunhofer-2018-lcoe_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-fraunhofer-2018-lcoe-40">[40]</a></sup><br/><i>Note:</i> employed technologies and LCOE differ by country and change over time.</div></div></div>
<p>In November 2013, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_Institute_for_Solar_Energy_Systems_ISE" title="Fraunhofer Institute for Solar Energy Systems ISE">Fraunhofer Institute for Solar Energy Systems ISE</a> assessed the levelised generation costs for newly built power plants in the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_Germany" title="Electricity sector in Germany">German electricity sector</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraunhofer-2013-lcoe_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-fraunhofer-2013-lcoe-41">[41]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PV_system" title="PV system">PV systems</a> reached LCOE between 0.078 and 0.142 Euro/kWh in the third quarter of 2013, depending on the type of power plant (<a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">ground-mounted utility-scale</a> or small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rooftop_solar_PV" title="Rooftop solar PV">rooftop solar PV</a>) and average German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a> of 1000 to 1200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a>/<a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">m²</a> per year (GHI). There are no LCOE-figures available for electricity generated by recently built <a href="/wiki/List_of_power_stations_in_Germany#Nuclear" title="List of power stations in Germany">German nuclear power plants</a> as none have been constructed since the late 1980s. An update of the ISE study was published in March 2018<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraunhofer-2018-lcoe_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-fraunhofer-2018-lcoe-40">[40]</a></sup>.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(July 2016)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A 2010 study by the Japanese government (pre-Fukushima disaster), called the Energy White Paper,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> concluded the cost for kilowatt hour was ¥49 for solar, ¥10 to ¥14 for wind, and ¥5 or ¥6 for nuclear power. <a href="/wiki/Masayoshi_Son" title="Masayoshi Son">Masayoshi Son</a>, an advocate for renewable energy, however, has pointed out that the government estimates for nuclear power did not include the costs for reprocessing the fuel or disaster insurance liability. Son estimated that if these costs were included, the cost of nuclear power was about the same as wind power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Institution of Engineers and Shipbuilders in Scotland commissioned a former Director of Operations of the British National Grid, Colin Gibson, to produce a report on generation levelised costs that for the first time would include some of the transmission costs as well as the generation costs. This was published in December 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The institution seeks to encourage debate of the issue, and has taken the unusual step among compilers of such studies of publishing a spreadsheet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 27 February 2015 Vattenfall Vindkraft AS agreed to build the Horns Rev 3 offshore wind farm at a price of <a class="external text" href="http://www.ens.dk/en/supply/renewable-energy/wind-power/offshore-wind-power/large-scale-offshore-wind-tenders" rel="nofollow">10.31 Eurocent per kWh</a>. This has been quoted as below <a class="external text" href="http://blogs.dnvgl.com/utilityofthefuture/100mwh-and-falling-dawn-breaks-on-a-cheaper-stronger-offshore-wind-industry" rel="nofollow">£100</a> per MWh.
</p><p>In 2013 in the United Kingdom for a new-to-build nuclear power plant (<a href="/wiki/Hinkley_Point_C_nuclear_power_station" title="Hinkley Point C nuclear power station">Hinkley Point C</a>: completion 2023), a feed-in tariff of £92.50/MWh (around 142 USD/MWh) plus compensation for inflation with a running time of 35 years was agreed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="BEIS">BEIS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: BEIS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Business,_Energy_and_Industrial_Strategy" title="Department for Business, Energy and Industrial Strategy">Department for Business, Energy and Industrial Strategy</a> (BEIS) publishes regular estimates of the costs of different electricity generation sources, following on the estimates of the merged <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Energy_and_Climate_Change" title="Department of Energy and Climate Change">Department of Energy and Climate Change</a> (DECC). Levelised cost estimates for new generation projects begun in 2015 are listed in the table below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEISnov2016_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEISnov2016-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_Information_Administration">Energy Information Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Energy Information Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:642px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_(as_of_2015).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1296" height="420" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_%28as_of_2015%29.png/640px-Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_%28as_of_2015%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_%28as_of_2015%29.png/960px-Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_%28as_of_2015%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_%28as_of_2015%29.png/1280px-Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_%28as_of_2015%29.png 2x" width="640"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projected_LCOE_in_the_U.S._by_2020_(as_of_2015).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Projected LCOE in the U.S. by 2020 (as of 2015) in dollars per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt-hour" title="Megawatt-hour">MWh</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The following data are from the Energy Information Administration's (EIA) Annual Energy Outlook released in 2015 (AEO2015). They are in dollars per megawatt-hour (2013 USD/MWh). These figures are estimates for plants going into service in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eia.gov_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-eia.gov-10">[10]</a></sup> The LCOE below is calculated based off a 30-year recovery period using a real after tax weighted average cost of capital (WACC) of 6.1%. For carbon intensive technologies 3 percentage points are added to the WACC. (This is approximately equivalent fee of $15 per metric ton of carbon dioxide CO<sub>2</sub>)
</p><p>Since 2010, the US Energy Information Administration (EIA) has published the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annual_Energy_Outlook" title="Annual Energy Outlook">Annual Energy Outlook</a> (AEO), with yearly LCOE-projections for future utility-scale facilities to be commissioned in about five years' time. In 2015, EIA has been criticized by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_Energy_Economy" title="Advanced Energy Economy">Advanced Energy Economy</a> (AEE) Institute after its release of the AEO 2015-report to "consistently underestimate the growth rate of renewable energy, leading to 'misperceptions' about the performance of these resources in the marketplace". AEE points out that the average <a href="/wiki/Power_purchase_agreement" title="Power purchase agreement">power purchase agreement</a> (PPA) for wind power was already at $24/MWh in 2013. Likewise, PPA for utility-scale <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_PV" title="Solar PV">solar PV</a> are seen at current levels of $50–$75/MWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> These figures contrast strongly with EIA's estimated LCOE of $125/MWh (or $114/MWh including subsidies) for solar PV in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eia-aeo-2015_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-eia-aeo-2015-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The electricity sources which had the most decrease in estimated costs over the period 2010 to 2017 were solar photovoltaic (down 81%), onshore wind (down 63%) and advanced natural gas combined cycle (down 32%).
</p><p>For utility-scale generation put into service in 2040, the EIA estimated in 2015 that there would be further reductions in the constant-dollar cost of concentrated solar power (CSP) (down 18%), solar photovoltaic (down 15%), offshore wind (down 11%), and advanced nuclear (down 7%). The cost of onshore wind was expected to rise slightly (up 2%) by 2040, while natural gas combined cycle electricity was expected to increase 9% to 10% over the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eia-aeo-2015_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-eia-aeo-2015-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span id="NREL_OpenEI_.282015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="NREL_OpenEI_(2015)">NREL OpenEI (2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: NREL OpenEI (2015)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/OpenEI" title="OpenEI">OpenEI</a>, sponsored jointly by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Energy" title="US Department of Energy">US DOE</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), has compiled a historical cost-of-generation database<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-openei_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-openei-63">[63]</a></sup> covering a wide variety of generation sources. Because the data is open source it may be subject to frequent revision.
</p>

<h4><span id="California_Energy_Commission_.282014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="California_Energy_Commission_(2014)">California Energy Commission (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: California Energy Commission (2014)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<h4><span id="Lazard_.282015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lazard_(2015)">Lazard (2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Lazard (2015)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In November 2015, the investment bank <a href="/wiki/Lazard" title="Lazard">Lazard</a> headquartered in New York, published its ninth annual study on the current electricity production costs of photovoltaics in the US compared to conventional power generators. The best large-scale photovoltaic power plants can produce electricity at 50 USD per MWh. The upper limit at 60 USD per MWh. In comparison, coal-fired plants are between 65 USD and $150 per MWh, nuclear power at 97 USD per MWh. Small photovoltaic power plants on roofs of houses are still at 184–300 USD per MWh, but which can do without electricity transport costs. Onshore wind turbines are 32–77 USD per MWh. One drawback is the intermittency of solar and wind power. The study suggests a solution in <a href="/wiki/Battery_storage_power_station" title="Battery storage power station">batteries as a storage</a>, but these are still expensive so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lazard1_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-lazard1-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lazard's long standing Levelized Cost of Energy (LCOE) report is widely considered and industry benchmark.  In 2015 Lazard published its inaugural Levelized Cost of Storage (LCOS) report, which was developed by the investment bank Lazard in collaboration with the energy consulting firm, Enovation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Below is the complete list of LCOEs by source from the investment bank Lazard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lazard1_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-lazard1-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
