<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global">Global</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Global">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="IEA_and_NEA_.282015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="IEA_and_NEA_(2015)">IEA and NEA (2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: IEA and NEA (2015)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Energy_Agency" title="Nuclear Energy Agency">Nuclear Energy Agency</a> published a joint study in 2015 on LCOE data internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iea-nea-oecd-2015-summary_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-iea-nea-oecd-2015-summary-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iea-nea-oecd-2015-main_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-iea-nea-oecd-2015-main-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_studies_and_analysis">Other studies and analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Other studies and analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Buffett_Contract_.282015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Buffett_Contract_(2015)">Buffett Contract (2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Buffett Contract (2015)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In a power purchase agreement in the United States in July 2015 for a period of 20 years of solar power will be paid 3.87 UScent per kilowatt hour (38.7 USD/MWh). The solar system, which produces this solar power, is in Nevada (USA) and has 100 MW capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Sheikh_Mohammed_Bin_Rashid_solar_farm_.282016.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sheikh_Mohammed_Bin_Rashid_solar_farm_(2016)">Sheikh Mohammed Bin Rashid solar farm (2016)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Sheikh Mohammed Bin Rashid solar farm (2016)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the spring of 2016 a winning bid of 2.99 US cents per kilowatt-hour of photovoltaic solar energy was achieved for the next (800MW capacity) phase of the Sheikh Mohammed Bin Rashid solar farm in Dubai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Brookings_Institution_.282014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Brookings_Institution_(2014)">Brookings Institution (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Brookings Institution (2014)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> published <i>The Net Benefits of Low and No-Carbon Electricity Technologies</i> which states, after performing an energy and emissions cost analysis, that "The net benefits of new nuclear, hydro, and natural gas combined cycle plants far outweigh the net benefits of new wind or solar plants", with the most cost effective <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power" title="Low carbon power">low carbon power</a> technology being determined to be nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Brazilian_electricity_mix:_the_Renewable_and_Non-renewable_Exergetic_Cost_.282014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazilian_electricity_mix:_the_Renewable_and_Non-renewable_Exergetic_Cost_(2014)">Brazilian electricity mix: the Renewable and Non-renewable Exergetic Cost (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Brazilian electricity mix: the Renewable and Non-renewable Exergetic Cost (2014)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="769" data-file-width="1184" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff/lossy-page1-500px-Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff/lossy-page1-750px-Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff/lossy-page1-1000px-Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff.jpg 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Exergy_costs_of_Integrated_Brazilian_Electricity_Mix.tiff" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exergy costs of Integrated Brazilian Electricity Mix</div></div></div>
<p>As long as exergy stands for the useful energy required for an economic activity to be accomplished, it is reasonable to evaluate the cost of the energy on the basis of its exergy content. Besides, as exergy can be considered as measure of the departure of the environmental conditions, it also serves as an indicator of environmental impact, taking into account both the efficiency of supply chain (from primary exergy inputs) and the efficiency of the production processes. In this way, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Exergoeconomy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Exergoeconomy (page does not exist)">exergoeconomy</a> can be used to rationally distribute the exergy costs and CO<sub>2</sub> emission cost among the products and by-products of a highly integrated Brazilian electricity mix. Based on the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Thermoeconomy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Thermoeconomy (page does not exist)">thermoeconomy</a> methodologies, some authors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-80">[80]</a></sup> have shown that exergoeconomy provides an opportunity to quantify the renewable and non-renewable specific exergy consumption; to properly allocate the associated CO<sub>2</sub> emissions among the streams of a given production route; as well as to determine the overall exergy conversion efficiency of the production processes. Accordingly, the non-renewable unit exergy cost (cNR) [kJ/kJ] is defined as the rate of non-renewable exergy necessary to produce one unit of exergy rate/flow rate of a substance, fuel, electricity, work or heat flow, whereas the Total Unit Exergy Cost (cT) includes the Renewable (cR) and Non-Renewable Unit Exergy Costs. Analogously, the CO<sub>2</sub> emission cost (cCO<sub>2</sub>) [gCO<sub>2</sub>/kJ] is defined as the rate of CO<sub>2</sub> emitted to obtain one unit of exergy rate/flow rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewables">Renewables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Renewables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photovoltaics">Photovoltaics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Photovoltaics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="586" data-file-width="817" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png/220px-EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png/330px-EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png/440px-EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>European <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">PV</a> LCOE range projection 2010–2020 (in €-cts/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="649" data-file-width="729" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg/220px-Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg/330px-Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg/440px-Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Price_history_of_silicon_PV_cells_since_1977.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Price history of silicon PV cells since 1977</div></div></div>
<p>Photovoltaic prices have fallen from $76.67 per watt in 1977 to nearly $0.23 per watt in August 2017, for <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-energytrend-pv-spotprice_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-energytrend-pv-spotprice-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Econ1_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Econ1-83">[83]</a></sup> This is seen as evidence supporting <a href="/wiki/Swanson%27s_law" title="Swanson's law">Swanson's law</a>, which states that solar cell prices fall 20% for every doubling of cumulative shipments. The famous <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore's law">Moore's law</a> calls for a doubling of transistor count every two years.
</p><p>By 2011, the price of PV modules per MW had fallen by 60% since 2008, according to Bloomberg New Energy Finance estimates, putting solar power for the first time on a competitive footing with the retail price of electricity in some sunny countries; an alternative and consistent price decline figure of 75% from 2007 to 2012 has also been published,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wells2012_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-wells2012-84">[84]</a></sup> though it is unclear whether these figures are specific to the United States or generally global. The levelised cost of electricity (LCOE) from PV is competitive with conventional electricity sources in an expanding list of geographic regions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Branker_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Branker-6">[6]</a></sup> particularly when the time of generation is included, as electricity is worth more during the day than at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> There has been fierce competition in the supply chain, and further improvements in the levelised cost of energy for solar lie ahead, posing a growing threat to the dominance of fossil fuel generation sources in the next few years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-invest2011_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-invest2011-86">[86]</a></sup> As time progresses, renewable energy technologies generally get cheaper,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> while fossil fuels generally get more expensive:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The less solar power costs, the more favorably it compares to conventional power, and the more attractive it becomes to utilities and energy users around the globe. Utility-scale solar power [could in 2011] be delivered in California at prices well below $100/MWh ($0.10/kWh) less than most other peak generators, even those running on low-cost natural gas. Lower solar module costs also stimulate demand from consumer markets where the cost of solar compares very favourably to retail electric rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In the year 2015, <a href="/wiki/First_Solar" title="First Solar">First Solar</a> agreed to supply solar power at 3.87 cents/kWh levelised price from its 100 MW Playa Solar 2 project which is far cheaper than the electricity sale price from conventional electricity generation plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>  From January 2015 through May 2016, records have continued to fall quickly, and solar electricity prices, which have reached levels below 3 cents/kWh, continue to fall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>  In August 2016, Chile announced a new record low contract price to provide solar power for $29.10 per megawatt-hour (MWh).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In September 2016, Abu Dhabi announced a new record breaking bid price, promising to provide solar power for $24.2 per MWh<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In October 2017, Saudi Arabia announced a further low contract price to provide solar power for $17.90 per MWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>With a carbon price of $50/ton (which would raise the price of coal-fired power by 5c/kWh), solar PV is cost-competitive in most locations. The declining price of PV has been reflected in rapidly growing installations, totaling a worldwide cumulative capacity of 297 GW by end 2016. According to some estimates total investment in renewables for 2011 exceeded investment in carbon-based electricity generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natint_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-natint-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the case of self consumption, payback time is calculated based on how much electricity is not brought from the grid. Additionally, using PV solar power to charge DC batteries, as used in Plug-in Hybrid Electric Vehicles and Electric Vehicles, leads to greater efficiencies, but higher costs. Traditionally, DC generated electricity from solar PV must be converted to AC for buildings, at an average 10% loss during the conversion. Inverter technology is rapidly improving and current equipment has reached 99% efficiency for small scale residential,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> while commercial scale three-phase equipment can reach well above 98% efficiency. However, an additional efficiency loss occurs in the transition back to DC for battery driven devices and vehicles, and using various interest rates and energy price changes were calculated to find present values that range from $2,057.13 to $8,213.64 (analysis from 2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is also possible to combine solar PV with other technologies to make hybrid systems, which enable more stand alone systems. The calculation of LCOEs becomes more complex, but can be done by aggregating the costs and the energy produced by each component. As for example, PV and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cogen" title="Cogen">cogen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> while reducing energy- and electricity-related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> as compared to conventional sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_thermal">Solar thermal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Solar thermal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>LCOE of <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">solar thermal power</a> with energy storage which can operate round the clock on demand, has fallen to AU$78/MWh (US$61/MWh) in August 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Though solar thermal plants with energy storage can work as stand alone systems, combination with <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">solar PV</a> power can deliver further cheaper power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Cheaper and dispatchable solar thermal storage power need not depend on costly or polluting coal/gas/oil/nuclear based power generation for ensuring stable grid operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>When a solar thermal storage plant is forced to idle due to lack of sunlight locally during cloudy days, it is possible to consume the cheap excess infirm power from solar PV, wind and hydro power plants (similar to a lesser efficient, huge capacity and low cost battery storage system) by heating the hot molten salt to higher temperature for converting the stored thermal energy in to electricity during the peak demand hours when the electricity sale price is profitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_power">Wind power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Wind power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_projected_cost_of_wind_power.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="858" height="98" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/US_projected_cost_of_wind_power.png/225px-US_projected_cost_of_wind_power.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/US_projected_cost_of_wind_power.png/338px-US_projected_cost_of_wind_power.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/US_projected_cost_of_wind_power.png/450px-US_projected_cost_of_wind_power.png 2x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">NREL</a> projection: the LCOE of <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">U.S. wind power</a> will decline by 25% from 2012 to 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Danish_wind_power_LCOE_vs_wind_speed_in_2012.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="506" data-file-width="844" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Danish_wind_power_LCOE_vs_wind_speed_in_2012.png/225px-Danish_wind_power_LCOE_vs_wind_speed_in_2012.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Danish_wind_power_LCOE_vs_wind_speed_in_2012.png/338px-Danish_wind_power_LCOE_vs_wind_speed_in_2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Danish_wind_power_LCOE_vs_wind_speed_in_2012.png/450px-Danish_wind_power_LCOE_vs_wind_speed_in_2012.png 2x" width="225"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Estimated cost per MWh for <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Denmark" title="Wind power in Denmark">wind power in Denmark</a> as of 2012</div></div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Current land-based wind</dt></dl>
<p>In the windy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_plains" title="Great plains">great plains</a> expanse of the central <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> new-construction wind power costs in 2017 are compellingly below costs of continued use of existing coal burning plants.  Wind power can be contracted via a <a href="/wiki/Power_purchase_agreement" title="Power purchase agreement">power purchase agreement</a> at two cents per kilowatt hour while the operating costs for power generation in existing coal-burning plants remain above three cents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Current offshore wind</dt></dl>
<p>In 2016 the Norwegian Wind Energy Association (NORWEA) estimated the LCoE of a typical Norwegian wind farm at 44 €/MWh, assuming a weighted average cost of capital of 8% and an annual 3,500 full load hours, i.e. a capacity factor of 40%. NORWEA went on to estimate the LCoE of the 1 GW <a href="/wiki/Fosen_Vind" title="Fosen Vind">Fosen Vind</a> onshore wind farm which is expected to be operational by 2020 to be as low as 35 €/MWh to 40 €/MWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In November 2016, Vattenfall won a tender to develop the <a href="/wiki/Kriegers_Flak_(wind_farm)" title="Kriegers Flak (wind farm)">Kriegers Flak</a> windpark in the Baltic Sea for 49.9 €/MWh,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and similar levels were agreed for the <a href="/wiki/Borssele" title="Borssele">Borssele</a> offshore wind farms. As of 2016, this is the lowest projected price for electricity produced using offshore wind.
</p>
<dl><dt>Historic levels</dt></dl>
<p>In 2004, wind energy cost a fifth of what it did in the 1980s, and some expected that downward trend to continue as larger multi-megawatt <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">turbines</a> were mass-produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-helming_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-helming-110">[110]</a></sup> As of  2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cost_of_electricity_by_source&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> capital costs for wind turbines are substantially lower than 2008–2010 but are still above 2002 levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> A 2011 report from the American Wind Energy Association stated, "Wind's costs have dropped over the past two years, in the range of 5 to 6 cents per kilowatt-hour recently.... about 2 cents cheaper than coal-fired electricity, and more projects were financed through debt arrangements than tax equity structures last year.... winning more mainstream acceptance from Wall Street's banks.... Equipment makers can also deliver products in the same year that they are ordered instead of waiting up to three years as was the case in previous cycles.... 5,600 MW of new installed capacity is under construction in the United States, more than double the number at this point in 2010. 35% of all new power generation built in the United States since 2005 has come from wind, more than new gas and coal plants combined, as power providers are increasingly enticed to wind as a convenient hedge against unpredictable commodity price moves."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salerno_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-salerno-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>This cost has additionally reduced as wind turbine technology has improved. There are now longer and lighter wind turbine blades, improvements in turbine performance and increased power generation efficiency. Also, wind project capital and maintenance costs have continued to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> For example, the wind industry in the USA in 2014 was able to produce more power at lower cost by using taller wind turbines with longer blades, capturing the faster winds at higher elevations. This opened up new opportunities in Indiana, Michigan, and Ohio. The price of power from wind turbines built 300 to 400 ft (91 to 122 m) above the ground can now compete with conventional fossil fuels like coal. Prices have fallen to about 4 cents per kilowatt-hour in some cases and utilities have been increasing the amount of wind energy in their portfolio, saying it is their cheapest option.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Bronski2014-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bronski2014_8-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Bronski, Peter (29 May 2014). <a class="external text" href="https://www.webcitation.org/6laUWWL2y?url=http://blog.rmi.org/blog_2014_05_29_you_down_with_lcoe" rel="nofollow">"You Down With LCOE? Maybe You, But Not Me:Leaving behind the limitations of levelized cost of energy for a better energy metric"</a>. <i>RMI Outlet</i>. Rocky Mountain Institute (RMI). Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://blog.rmi.org/blog_2014_05_29_you_down_with_lcoe" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 28 October 2016<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 October</span> 2016</span>. <q>Desirable shifts in how we as a nation and as individual consumers—whether a residential home or commercial real estate property—manage, produce, and consume electricity can actually make LCOE numbers look worse, not better. This is particularly true when considering the influence of energy efficiency...If you’re planning a new, big central power plant, you want to get the best value (i.e., lowest LCOE) possible. For the cost of any given power-generating asset, that comes through maximizing the number of kWh it cranks out over its economic lifetime, which runs exactly counter to the highly cost-effective energy efficiency that has been a driving force behind the country’s flat and even declining electricity demand. On the flip side, planning new big, central power plants without taking continued energy efficiency gains (of which there’s no shortage of opportunity—the February 2014 UNEP Finance Initiative report Commercial Real Estate: Unlocking the energy efficiency retrofit investment opportunity identified a $231–$300 billion annual market by 2020) into account risks overestimating the number of kWh we’d need from them and thus lowballing their LCOE... If I’m a homeowner or business considering purchasing rooftop solar outright, do I care more about the per-unit value (LCOE) or my total out of pocket (lifetime system cost)?...The per-unit value is less important than the thing considered as a whole...LCOE, for example, fails to take into account the time of day during which an asset can produce power, where it can be installed on the grid, and its carbon intensity, among many other variables. That’s why, in addition to [levelized avoided cost of energy (LACE)], utilities and other electricity system stakeholders...have used benefit/cost calculations and/or an asset’s capacity value or contribution to peak on a system or circuit level.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=RMI+Outlet&amp;rft.atitle=You+Down+With+LCOE%3F+Maybe+You%2C+But+Not+Me%3ALeaving+behind+the+limitations+of+levelized+cost+of+energy+for+a+better+energy+metric&amp;rft.date=2014-05-29&amp;rft.aulast=Bronski&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fblog.rmi.org%2Fblog_2014_05_29_you_down_with_lcoe&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACost+of+electricity+by+source"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">US Energy Information Administration, <a class="external text" href="https://www.eia.gov/forecasts/aeo/pdf/electricity_generation.pdf" rel="nofollow">Levelized cost of new generation resources</a>, 28 January 2013.</span>

Energy Efficiency Standards (DOE).</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Marginal+Energy+Price+Analyses&amp;rft.pub=Energy+Efficiency+Standards+%28DOE%29&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fees.lbl.gov%2Fcapability%2Fmarginal-energy-price-analyses&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACost+of+electricity+by+source"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-eu2012-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-eu2012_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-eu2012_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"<a class="external text" href="http://ec.europa.eu/energy/studies/doc/20141013_subsidies_costs_eu_energy.pdf" rel="nofollow">Subsidies and costs of EU energy. Project number: DESNL14583</a>" Pages: 52. <i>EcoFys</i>, 10 October 2014. Accessed: 20 October 2014. Size: 70 pages in 2MB.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.eia.gov/forecasts/ieo/electricity.cfm" rel="nofollow">International Energy Outlook: Electricity</a> "Although coal-fired generation increases by an annual average of only 1.9 percent, it remains the largest source of electricity generation through 2035. In 2008, coal-fired generation accounted for 40 percent of world electricity supply; in 2035, its share decreases to 37 percent, as renewables, natural gas, and nuclear power all are expected to advance strongly during the projection and displace the need for coal-fired-generation in many parts of the world. World net coal-fired generation grows by 67 percent, from 7.7 trillion kilowatthours in 2008 to 12.9 trillion kilowatthours in 2035." <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120822160924/http://www.eia.gov/forecasts/ieo/electricity.cfm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> August 22, 2012, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Danielson, David (14 August 2012). <a class="external text" href="http://www.whitehouse.gov/blog/2012/08/14/banner-year-us-wind-industry" rel="nofollow">"A Banner Year for the U.S. Wind Industry"</a>. <i>Whitehouse Blog</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Whitehouse+Blog&amp;rft.atitle=A+Banner+Year+for+the+U.S.+Wind+Industry&amp;rft.date=2012-08-14&amp;rft.au=Danielson%2C+David&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.whitehouse.gov%2Fblog%2F2012%2F08%2F14%2Fbanner-year-us-wind-industry&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACost+of+electricity+by+source"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
